            "content": "1. <b>Br─Бhmaс╣Зa,</b>┬ж тАШdescendant of a BrahmanтАЩ (<i>i.e.,</i> of a priest), is found only a few times in the Rigveda,<sup>1</sup> and mostly in its latest parts. In the Atharvaveda<sup>2</sup> and later<sup>3</sup> it is a very common word denoting тАШpriest,тАЩ and it appears in the quadruple division of the castes in the Puruс╣гa-s┼лkta (тАШhymn of manтАЩ) of the Rigveda.<sup>4</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>It seems certain that in the Rigveda this Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, or Brahmin, is already a separate caste, differing from the warrior and agricultural castes.<sup>5</sup> The texts regularly claim for them a superiority to the <b>Kс╣гatriya</b> caste,<sup>6</sup> and the Brahmin is able by his spells or manipulation of the rite to embroil the people and the warriors<sup>7</sup> or the different sections of the warriors.<sup>8</sup> If it is necessary to recognize, as is sometimes done, that the Brahmin does pay homage to the king at the <b>R─Бjas┼лya,</b><sup>9</sup> nevertheless the unusual fact is carefully explained away so as to leave the priority of the Brahmin unaffected. But it is expressly recognized that the union of the Kс╣гatriya and the Br─Бhmaс╣Зa is essential for complete prosperity.<sup>10</sup> It is admitted<sup>11</sup> that the king or the nobles might at times oppress the Brahmins, but it is indicated that ruin is then certain swiftly to follow. <dvsn n='P'/>The Brahmins are gods on earth,<sup>12</sup> like the gods in heaven, but this claim is hardly found in the Rigveda.<sup>13</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>In the Aitareya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>14</sup> the Brahmin is said to be the тАШrecipient of giftsтАЩ (<i>─Бd─Бy─л</i>) and the тАШdrinker of the offeringтАЩ (<i>─Бp─Бy─л</i>). The other two epithets applied, <i>─Бvas─Бy─л</i> and <i>yath─Б-</i> <i>k─Бma-pray─Бpya,</i> are more obscure; the former denotes either тАШdwelling everywhereтАЩ<sup>15</sup> or тАШseeking foodтАЩ;<sup>16</sup> the latter is usually taken as тАШmoving at pleasure,тАЩ but it must rather allude to the power of the king to assign a place of residence to the Brahmin. <dvsn n='P'/>In the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>17</sup> the prerogatives of the Brahmin are summed up as (1) Arc─Б, тАШhonourтАЩ; (2) D─Бna, тАШgiftsтАЩ; (3) Ajyeyat─Б, тАШfreedom from oppressionтАЩ; and (4) Avadhyat─Б, тАШfreedom from being killed.тАЩ On the other hand, his duties are summed up as (5) Br─Бhmaс╣Зya, тАШpurity of descentтАЩ; (6) Pratir┼лpa-cary─Б, тАШdevotion of the duties of his casteтАЩ; and (7) Loka-pakti, тАШthe perfecting of peopleтАЩ (by teaching). <dvsn n='P'/>1. <i>Respect paid to Brahmins.</i> тАФ The texts are full<sup>18</sup> of references to the civilities to be paid to the Brahmin. He is styled <i>bhagavant,</i><sup>19</sup> and is provided with good food<sup>20</sup> and entertainment wherever he goes. Indeed, his sanctity exempts him from any close inquiry into his real claim to Brahminhood according to the Pa├▒caviс╣Г┼Ыa Br─Бhmaс╣Зa.<sup>21</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>2. <i>Gifts to Brahmins.</i> тАФ The D─Бnastuti (тАШPraise of giftsтАЩ) is a recognized feature of the Rigveda, and the greed of the poets for <b>Dakс╣гiс╣З─Бs,</b> or sacrificial fees, is notorious. Vedic texts<sup>22</sup> themselves recognize that the literature thence resulting (<b>N─Бr─Б-</b> <b>┼Ыaс╣Гs─л</b>) was often false to please the donors. It was, however, a rule<sup>23</sup> that Brahmins should not accept what had been refused by others; this indicates a keen sense of the danger of cheapening their wares. So exclusively theirs was the right to receive gifts that the Pa├▒caviс╣Г┼Ыa Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>24</sup> has to explain how <b>Taranta</b> and <b>Purum─лс╕╖ha</b> became able to accept gifts by composing a Rigvedic hymn.<sup>25</sup> The exaggerations in the celebration of the gifts bestowed on the priests has the curious result of giving us a series of numerals of some interest (<b>Da┼Ыan</b>). In some passages<sup>26</sup> certain gifts тАФ those of a horse or sheep тАФ are forbidden, but this rule was not, it is clear, generally observed. <dvsn n='P'/>3. <i>Immunities of Brahmins.</i> тАФ The Brahmin claimed to be exempt from the ordinary exercise of the royal power. When a king gives all his land and what is on it to the priests, the gift does not cover the property of the Brahmin according to the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa.<sup>27</sup> The king censures all, but not the Brahmin,<sup>28</sup> nor can he safely oppress any Brahmin other than an ignorant priest.<sup>29</sup> An arbitrator (or a witness) must decide (or speak) for a Brahmin against a non-Brahmin in a legal dispute.<sup>30</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>The Brahmin's proper food is the <b>Soma,</b><sup>31</sup> not <b>Sur─Б</b><sup>32</sup> or <b>Parisrut,</b><sup>33</sup> and he is forbidden to eat certain forms of flesh.<sup>34</sup> On the other hand, he alone is allowed to eat the remains of the sacrifice,<sup>35</sup> for no one else is sufficiently holy to consume food which the gods have eaten. Moreover, though he cannot be a physician,<sup>36</sup> he helps the physician by being beside him while he exercises his art.<sup>37</sup> His wife<sup>38</sup> and his cow<sup>39</sup> are both sacred. <dvsn n='P'/>4. <i>Legal Position of Brahmins.</i> тАФ The Taittir─лya Saс╣Гhit─Б<sup>40</sup> lays down a penalty of a hundred (the unit meant is unknown) for an insult to a Brahmin, and of a thousand for a blow; but if his blood is drawn, the penalty is a spiritual one. The only real murder is the slaying of a Brahmin according to the Satapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa.<sup>41</sup> The crime of slaying a Brahmin ranks above the sin of killing any other man, but below that of killing an embryo (<i>bhr┼лс╣Зa</i>) in the Yajurveda;<sup>42</sup> the crime of slaying an embryo whose sex is uncertain is on a level with that of slaying a Brahmin.<sup>43</sup> The murder of a Brahmin can be expiated only by the horse sacrifice,<sup>44</sup> or by a lesser rite in the late Taittir─лya ─Аraс╣Зyaka.<sup>45</sup> The ritual slaying of a Brahmin is allowed in the later ceremonial,<sup>46</sup> and hinted at in the curious legend of <b>┼Ъunaс╕е┼Ыepa;</b><sup>47</sup> and a Purohita might be punished with death for treachery to his master.<sup>48</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>5. <i>Purity of Birth.</i> тАФ The importance of pure descent is seen in the stress laid on being a descendant of a с╣Ъс╣гi (<i>─Бrс╣гeya</i>).<sup>49</sup> But, on the other hand, there are clear traces of another doctrine, which requires learning, and not physical descent, as the true criterion of с╣Ъс╣гihood.<sup>50</sup> In agreement with this is the fact that <b>Satyak─Бma J─Бb─Бla</b> was received as a pupil, though his parentage was unknown, his mother being a slave girl who had been connected with several men,<sup>51</sup> and that in the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>52</sup> the ceremony on acceptance as a pupil required merely the name of the pupil. So <b>Kavaс╣гa</b> is taunted in the Rigveda Br─Бhmaс╣Зas<sup>53</sup> as being the son of a female slave (<b>D─Бs─л</b>), and <b>Vatsa</b> cleared himself of a similar imputation by a fire ordeal.<sup>54</sup> Moreover, a very simple rite was adequate to remove doubts as to origin.<sup>55</sup> In these circumstances it is doubtful whether much value attaches to the <b>Pravara</b> lists in which the ancestors of the priest were invoked at the beginning of the sacrifice by the Hotс╣Ы and the Adhvaryu priests.<sup>56</sup> Still, in many parts of the ritual the knowledge of two or more generations was needed,<sup>57</sup> and in one ceremony<sup>58</sup> ten ancestors who have drunk the Soma are required, but a literal performance of the rite is excused. Moreover, there are clear traces of ritual variations in schools, like those of the <b>Vasiс╣гс╣нhas</b> and the <b>Vi┼Ыv─Бmitras.</b> <dvsn n='P'/>6. <i>The Conduct of the Brahmin.</i> тАФ The Brahmin was required to maintain a fair standard of excellence.<sup>59</sup> He was to be kind to all<sup>60</sup> and gentle,<sup>61</sup> offering sacrifice and receiving gifts.<sup>62</sup> Especial stress was laid on purity of speech;<sup>63</sup> thus <b>Vi┼Ыvan-</b> <b>tara's</b> excuse for excluding the <b>┼Ъy─Бparс╣Зas</b> from his retinue was their impure (<i>ap┼лt─Б</i>) speech.<sup>64</sup> Theirs was the craving for knowledge<sup>65</sup> and the life of begging.<sup>66</sup> False Brahmins are those who do not fulfil their duties<sup>67</sup> (<i>cf.</i> <b>Brahmabandhu</b>) . But the penances for breach of duty are, in the S┼лtras, of a very light and unimportant character.<sup>68</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>7. <i>Brahminical Studies.</i> тАФ The aim of the priest is to obtain pre-eminence in sacred knowledge (<i>brahma-varcasam</i>), as is stated in numerous passages of Vedic literature.<sup>69</sup> Such distinction is not indeed confined to the Brahmin: the king has it also, but it is not really in a special manner appropriate to the Kс╣гatriya.<sup>70</sup> Many ritual acts are specified as leading to Brahmavarcasa,<sup>71</sup> but more stress is laid on the study of the sacred texts: the importance of such study is repeatedly insisted upon.<sup>72</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>The technical name for study is Sv─Бdhy─Бya: the Satapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa is eloquent upon its advantages,<sup>73</sup> and it is asserted that the joy of the learned ┼Ъrotriya, or тАШstudent,тАЩ is equal to the highest joy possible.<sup>74</sup> <b>N─Бka Maudgalya</b> held that study and the teaching of others were the true penance (<i>tapas</i>).<sup>75</sup> The object was the тАШthreefold knowledgeтАЩ (<i>tray─л vidy─Б</i>), that of the с╣Ъc, Yajus, and S─Бman,<sup>76</sup> a student of all three Vedas being called <i>tri-┼Ыukriya</i><sup>77</sup> or <i>tri-┼Ыukra,</i><sup>78</sup> тАШthrice pure.тАЩ Other objects of study are enumerated in the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa,<sup>79</sup> in the Taittir─лya ─Аraс╣Зyaka,<sup>80</sup> the Ch─Бndogya Upaniс╣гad,<sup>81</sup> etc. (See <b>Itih─Бsa, Pur─Бс╣Зa; G─Бth─Б, N─Бr─Б┼Ыaс╣Гs─л; Brahmodya; Anu┼Ы─Бs-</b> <b>ana, Anuvy─Бkhy─Бna, Anv─Бkhy─Бna, Kalpa,</b> 2. <b>Br─Бhmaс╣Зa;</b> <b>Vidy─Б, Kс╣гatravidy─Б, Devajanavidy─Б, Nakс╣гatravidy─Б, Bh┼лta-</b> <b>vidy─Б, Sarpavidy─Б; Atharv─Бс╣Еgirasaс╕е, Daiva, Nidhi, Pitrya,</b> <b>R─Б┼Ыi; S┼лtra,</b> etc.) <dvsn n='P'/>Directions as to the exact place and time of study are given in the Taittir─лya ─Аraс╣Зyaka<sup>82</sup> and in the S┼лtras. If study is carried on in the village, it is to be done silently (<i>manas─Б</i>); if outside, aloud (<i>v─Бc─Б</i>). <dvsn n='P'/>Learning is expected even from persons not normally competent as teachers, such as the <b>Carakas,</b> who are recognized in the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>83</sup> as possible sources of information. Here, too, may be mentioned the cases of Brahmins learning from princes, though their absolute value is doubtful, for the priests would naturally represent their patrons as interested in their sacred science: it is thus not necessary to see in these notices any real and independent study on the part of the Kс╣гatriyas.<sup>84</sup> <b>Y─Бj├▒avalkya</b> learnt from <b>Janaka,</b><sup>85</sup> <b>Udd─Бlaka</b> <b>─Аruс╣Зi</b> and two other Brahmins from <b>Prav─Бhaс╣Зa Jaivali,</b><sup>86</sup> <b>Dс╣Ыptab─Бl─Бki G─Бrgya</b> from <b>Aj─Бta┼Ыatru,</b><sup>87</sup> and five Brahmins under the lead of Aruс╣Зa from <b>A┼Ыvapati Kaikeya.</b><sup>88</sup> A few notices show the real educators of thought: wandering scholars went through the country<sup>89</sup> and engaged in disputes and discussions in which a prize was staked by the disputants.<sup>90</sup> Moreover, kings like Janaka offered rewards to the most learned of the Brahmins;<sup>91</sup> Aj─Бta┼Ыatru was jealous of his renown, and imitated his generosity. Again, learned women are several times mentioned in the Br─Бhmaс╣Зas.<sup>92</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>A special form of disputation was the <b>Brahmodya,</b> for which there was a regular place at the A┼Ыvamedha (тАШhorse sacrificeтАЩ)<sup>93</sup> and at the Da┼Ыar─Бtra (тАШten-day festivalтАЩ).<sup>94</sup> The reward of learning was the gaining of the title of Kavi or Vipra, тАШsage.тАЩ<sup>95</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>8. <i>The Functions of the Brahmin.</i> тАФ The Brahmin was required not merely to practise individual culture, but also to give others the advantage of his skill, either as a teacher or as a sacrificial priest, or as a Purohita. <dvsn n='P'/>As a teacher the Brahmin has, of course, the special duty of instructing his own son in both study and sacrificial ritual.<sup>96</sup> The texts give examples of this, such as ─Аruс╣Зi and <b>┼Ъvetaketu,</b><sup>97</sup> or mythically Varuс╣Зa and Bhс╣Ыgu.<sup>98</sup> This fact also appears from some of the names in the Vaс╣Г┼Ыa Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>99</sup> of the S─Бmaveda and the Vaс╣Г┼Ыa (list of teachers) of the ┼Ъ─Бс╣Еkh─Бyana ─Аraс╣Зyaka.<sup>100</sup> On the other hand, these Vaс╣Г┼Ыas and the Vaс╣Г┼Ыas of the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa show that a father often preferred to let his son study under a famous teacher. The relation of pupil and teacher is described under <b>Brahmacarya.</b> A teacher might take several pupils,<sup>101</sup> and he was bound to teach them with all his heart and soul.<sup>102</sup> He was bound to reveal everything to his pupil, at any rate to one who was staying with him for a year (<i>saс╣Гvatsara-v─Бsin</i>),<sup>103</sup> an expression which shows, as was natural, that a pupil might easily change teachers. But, nevertheless, certain cases of learning kept secret and only revealed to special persons are enumerated.<sup>104</sup> The exact times and modes of teaching are elaborately laid down in the S┼лtras,<sup>105</sup> but not in the earlier texts. <dvsn n='P'/>As priest the Brahmin operated in all the greater sacrifices; the simple domestic (<i>gс╣Ыhya</i>) rites could normally be performed without his help, but not the more important rites (<i>┼Ыrauta</i>). The number varied: the ritual literature requires sixteen priests to be employed at the greatest sacrifices (see <b>с╣Ъtvij</b>), but other rites could be accomplished with four,<sup>106</sup> five,<sup>107</sup> six,<sup>108</sup> seven,<sup>109</sup> or ten<sup>110</sup> priests. Again, the Kauс╣г─лtakins<sup>111</sup> had a seventeenth priest beside the usual sixteen, the Sadasya, so called because he watched the performance from the Sadas, тАШseat.тАЩ In one rite, the Sattra (тАШsacrificial sessionтАЩ) of the serpents, the Pa├▒caviс╣Г┼Ыa Br─Бhmaс╣Зa,<sup>112</sup> adds three more to the sixteen, a second Unnetс╣Ы, an Abhigara, and an Apagara. The later ritual places the Brahman at the head of all the priests, but this is probably not the early view (see <b>Brahman</b>). <dvsn n='P'/>The sacrifice ensured, if properly performed, primarily the advantages of the sacrificer (<i>yajam─Бna</i>),<sup>113</sup> but the priest shared in the profit, besides securing the <b>Dakс╣гiс╣З─Бs.</b> Disputes between sacrificers and the priests were not rare, as in the case of <b>Vi┼Ыvantara</b> and the <b>┼Ъy─Бparс╣Зas,</b><sup>114</sup> or <b>Janamejaya</b> and the <b>Asitamс╣Ыgas;</b><sup>115</sup> and the <b>Aiс╣г─Бv─лras</b> are referred to as undesirable priests.<sup>116</sup> Moreover, <b>Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra</b> once held the post of Purohita to <b>Sud─Бs,</b> but gave place to <b>Vasiс╣гс╣нha.</b> <dvsn n='P'/>The position of <b>Purohita</b> differed considerably from that of the ordinary priest, for the Purohita not merely might officiate at the sacrifice, but was the officiator in all the private sacrifices of his king. Hence he could, and undoubtedly sometimes did, obtain great influence over his master in matters of secular importance; and the power of the priesthood in political as opposed to domestic and religious matters, no doubt rested on the Purohita. <dvsn n='P'/>There is no recognition in Vedic literature of the rule later prevailing by which, after spending part of his life as a Brahmac─Бrin, and part as a householder, the Brahmin became an ascetic<sup>117</sup> (later divided into the two stages of V─Бnaprastha, тАШforest-dweller,тАЩ and Saс╣Гny─Бsin, тАШmysticтАЩ). Y─Бj├▒avalkya's case<sup>118</sup> shows that study of the Absolute might empty life of all its content for the sage, and drive him to abandon wife and family. In Buddhist times the same phenomenon is seen<sup>119</sup> applying to other than Brahmins. The Buddhist texts are here confirmed in some degree by the Greek authorities.<sup>120</sup> The practice bears a certain resemblance to the habit of kings, in the Epic tradition,<sup>121</sup> of retiring to the forest when active life is over. <dvsn n='P'/>From the Greek authorities<sup>122</sup> it also appears тАФ what is certainly the case in the Buddhist literature<sup>123</sup> тАФ that Brahmins practised the most diverse occupations. It is difficult to say how far this was true for the Vedic period. The analogy of the Druids<sup>124</sup> тАФ in some respects very close тАФ suggests that the Brahmins may have been mainly confined to their professional tasks, including all the learned professions such as astronomy<sup>125</sup> and so forth. This is not contradicted by any Vedic evidence, for instance, the poet of a hymn of the Rigveda<sup>126</sup> says he is a poet, his father a physician (<b>Bhiс╣гaj</b>), and his mother a grinder of corn (<b>Upala-prakс╣гiс╣З─л</b>). This would seem to show that a Brahmin could be a doctor, while his wife would perform the ordinary household duties. So a Purohita could perhaps take the field to assist the king by prayer, as Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra,<sup>127</sup> and later on Vasiс╣гс╣нha<sup>128</sup> do, but this does not show that priests normally fought. Nor do they seem normally to have been agriculturists or merchants. On the other hand, they kept cattle: a Brahmac─Бrin's duty was to watch his master's cattle.<sup>129</sup> It is therefore needless to suppose that they could not, and did not, on occasion turn to agricultural or mercantile pursuits, as they certainly did later. But it must be remembered that in all probability there was more purity of blood, and less pressure of life, among the Brahmins of the Vedic age than later in Buddhist times, when the Vedic sacrificial apparatus was falling into grave disrepute. <dvsn n='P'/>It is clear that the Brahmins, whatever their defects, represented the intellectual side of Vedic life, and that the Kс╣гatriyas, if they played a part in that life, did so only in a secondary degree, and to a minor extent. It is natural to suppose that the Brahmins also composed ballads, the precursors of the epic; for though none such have survived, a few stanzas of this character, celebrating the generosity of patrons, have been preserved by being embedded in priestly compositions. A legend in the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>130</sup> shows clearly that the Brahmins regarded civilization as being spread by them only: <b>Kosala</b> and <b>Videha,</b> no doubt settled by ─Аryan tribes, are only rendered civilized and habitable by the influence of pious Brahmins. We need not doubt that the non-Brahminical tribes (see <b>Vr─Бtya</b>) had attained intellectual as well as material civilization, but it is reasonable to assume that their civilization was inferior to that of the Brahmins, for the history of Hinduism is the conquest by the Brahmins тАФ not by arms, but by mind тАФ of the tribes ─Аryan and non-─Аryan originally beyond the pale. <F>1) i. 164, 45; vi. 75, 10; vii. 103, 1. 7. 8; x. 16, 6; 71, 8. 9; 88, 19; 90, 12; 97, 22; 109, 4.
